1️⃣ Brushing first thing in the morning and before bed is key. Since you produce less saliva overnight, bad bacteria can grow overnight. You want to clear that out before you eat or drink anything.

2️⃣ Tongue scraping first thing in the AM is the best, for the same reason as brushing. You want to clear that bad bacteria off the tongue from the previous night.

3️⃣ Studies show us that flossing before brushing is prefered if you have the choice. You end up loosening plaque prior to brushing.

4️⃣ If you use a waterpik do it before you brush for the same reason and sometimes you can choose to use it instead of floss.

5️⃣ Some people truly love oil pulling and in my opinion it would work best to do it after tongue scraping, but before you floss or brush, so you can thoroughly rinse.

6️⃣ I’m not a huge mouthwash fan, but if you do use it I would recommend using it prior to brushing also, because you want the toothpaste to sit on your teeth after you brush. This would be true for most toothpaste options. If you use mouthwash you end up washing this away.

[debug] ffmpeg command line: ffmpeg -y -loglevel repeat+info -i file:C:\Temp\yt-dlp\20241124194227.mp4 -map 0 -dn -ignore_unknown -c copy -write_id3v1 1 -metadata "title=Video by thejrecompanion" -metadata "date=20241124" -metadata "description=When 3.4% Was \"Fact\" and Trump Was \"Wrong\"... Until He Wasn’t

In March 2020, then-President Donald Trump faced intense backlash after questioning the World Health Organization’s 3.4% fatality rate estimate for COVID-19.

Trump predicted the actual death rate would be less than 1%, arguing that the higher figure failed to account for mild or asymptomatic cases.

His comments were widely mocked and criticized by media outlets and public figures, with many labeling him “MISINFORMED,” “IRRESPONSIBLE,” “IGNORANT,” and even “DANGEROUS” for allegedly downplaying the severity of the virus.

However, as more comprehensive data emerged, studies began to support Trump’s assertion.

Research from institutions like Stanford University showed the infection fatality rate (IFR) to be significantly lower than 3.4%, often falling below 1% when asymptomatic and undiagnosed cases were included.

Despite the widespread condemnation at the time, Trump’s prediction ultimately aligned with later findings, raising questions about the rush to label his statement as “FALSE” or “UNFOUNDED.”

                    : In March 2020, then-President Donald Trump faced intense backlash after questioning the World Health Organization’s 3.4% fatality rate estimate for COVID-19.
                    :
                    : Trump predicted the actual death rate would be less than 1%, arguing that the higher figure failed to account for mild or asymptomatic cases.
                    :
                    : His comments were widely mocked and criticized by media outlets and public figures, with many labeling him “MISINFORMED,” “IRRESPONSIBLE,” “IGNORANT,” and even “DANGEROUS” for allegedly downplaying the severity of the virus.
                    :
                    : However, as more comprehensive data emerged, studies began to support Trump’s assertion.
                    :
                    : Research from institutions like Stanford University showed the infection fatality rate (IFR) to be significantly lower than 3.4%, often falling below 1% when asymptomatic and undiagnosed cases were included.
                    :
                    : Despite the widespread condemnation at the time, Trump’s prediction ultimately aligned with later findings, raising questions about the rush to label his statement as “FALSE” or “UNFOUNDED.”
                    :
                    : Discussed On
                    : The Joe Rogan Experience
                    : Episode 2232 - Jimmy Corsetti & Dan Richards
